     <p><b>Texas A&amp;M University</b> (<b>A&amp;M</b> or <b>TAMU</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Coeducation" title="Coeducation" class="mw-redirect">coeducational</a> <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public</a> research university located in <a href="/wiki/College_Station,_Texas" title="College Station, Texas">College Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, United States. It is the <a href="/wiki/Flagship_campus" title="Flagship campus" class="mw-redirect">flagship</a> institution of the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_System" title="Texas A&amp;M University System">Texas A&amp;M University System</a>, the seventh-largest university in the United States and the largest university in Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Texas A&amp;M's designation as a <a href="/wiki/Land-grant_university" title="Land-grant university">land</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_grant_colleges" title="Sea grant colleges" class="mw-redirect">sea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Space_grant_colleges" title="Space grant colleges" class="mw-redirect">space grant</a> institution reflects a broad range of research with ongoing projects funded by agencies such as the <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a> (NASA), the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Naval_Research" title="Office of Naval Research">Office of Naval Research</a>. The school ranks in the top 20 American research institutes in terms of funding and has made notable contributions to such fields as <a href="/wiki/Animal_cloning" title="Animal cloning" class="mw-redirect">animal cloning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_engineering" title="Petroleum engineering">petroleum engineering</a>.</p> 
     <p>The first public institution of higher education in Texas, though not the first general university in the state, the school opened on October 4, 1876<sup id="cite_ref-Texas_A.26M_University_Fact_Sheet_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Texas_A.26M_University_Fact_Sheet-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> as the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Texas_A%26M_University" title="History of Texas A&amp;M University">Agricultural and Mechanical College of Texas</a>. Originally, the college taught no classes in agriculture, instead concentrating on classical studies, languages, literature, and applied mathematics. After four years, students could attain degrees in scientific agriculture, <a href="/wiki/Civil_engineering" title="Civil engineering">civil</a> and mining engineering, and language and literature.<sup id="cite_ref-dethloff18_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dethloff18-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Under the leadership of President <a href="/wiki/James_Earl_Rudder" title="James Earl Rudder">James Earl Rudder</a>, in the 1960s A&amp;M desegregated, became coeducational, and dropped the requirement for participation in the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_Corps_of_Cadets" title="Texas A&amp;M University Corps of Cadets">Corps of Cadets</a>. To reflect the institution's expanded roles and academic offerings, the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Legislature" title="Texas Legislature">Texas Legislature</a> renamed the school to Texas A&amp;M University in 1963. The letters &quot;A&amp;M&quot;, originally short for &quot;Agricultural and Mechanical&quot;, are retained only as a link to the university's past. The school's students, <a href="/wiki/Alumnus" title="Alumnus">alumni</a>, and sports teams are known as &quot;<a href="/wiki/Aggie_(disambiguation)#Schools" title="Aggie (disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect">Aggies</a>&quot;.</p> 
     <p>The main campus is one of the largest in America, spanning 5,500 acres (22&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-TAMUCatalog_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMUCatalog-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TAMUfacts_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMUfacts-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> and includes the <a href="/wiki/George_Bush_Presidential_Library" title="George Bush Presidential Library">George Bush Presidential Library</a>. Approximately one-fifth of the student body lives on campus. Texas A&amp;M has approximately 800 officially recognized student organizations. Many students also observe the <a href="/wiki/Traditions_of_Texas_A%26M_University" title="Traditions of Texas A&amp;M University">traditions of Texas A&amp;M University</a>, which govern daily life as well as special occasions, including sports events. On July 1, 2012, the school joined the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Conference" title="Southeastern Conference">Southeastern Conference</a>. A&amp;M operates two branches: <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_at_Qatar" title="Texas A&amp;M University at Qatar">Texas A&amp;M at Qatar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_at_Galveston" title="Texas A&amp;M University at Galveston">Texas A&amp;M University at Galveston</a>. Working with agencies such as the <a href="/wiki/Texas_AgriLife_Research" title="Texas AgriLife Research">Texas AgriLife Research</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas_AgriLife_Extension_Service" title="Texas AgriLife Extension Service" class="mw-redirect">Texas AgriLife Extension Service</a>, Texas A&amp;M has a direct presence in each of the 254 counties in Texas. The university offers degrees in over 150 courses of study through ten colleges and houses 18 research institutes. Texas A&amp;M has awarded over 320,000 degrees, including 70,000 graduate and professional degrees.</p> 
     <p>As a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senior_Military_College" title="United States Senior Military College">Senior Military College</a>, Texas A&amp;M is one of three public universities with a full-time, volunteer Corps of Cadets.</p> 
     <table id="toc" class="toc">

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Texas_A%26M_University" title="History of Texas A&amp;M University">History of Texas A&amp;M University</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:TexasA%26MCampus_1902.jpg" class="image"><img alt="It shows, left to right, Ross Hall, Old Main, and Foster Hall." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/TexasA%26MCampus_1902.jpg/220px-TexasA%26MCampus_1902.jpg" width="220" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/TexasA%26MCampus_1902.jpg/330px-TexasA%26MCampus_1902.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/TexasA%26MCampus_1902.jpg/440px-TexasA%26MCampus_1902.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:TexasA%26MCampus_1902.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Texas A&amp;M in 1902
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_years">Beginning years</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Beginning years">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" title="U.S. Congress" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Congress</a> laid the groundwork for the establishment of Texas A&amp;M in 1862 with the adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Morrill_Act" title="Morrill Act" class="mw-redirect">Morrill Act</a>. The act auctioned land grants of public lands to establish endowments for colleges where the &quot;leading object shall be, without excluding other scientific and classical studies and including military tactics, to teach such branches of learning as are related to agriculture and mechanical arts... to promote the liberal and practical education of the industrial classes in the several pursuits and professions in life&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1871, the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Legislature" title="Texas Legislature">Texas Legislature</a> used these funds to establish the state's first public institution of higher education,<sup id="cite_ref-txconstitution_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-txconstitution-20"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> the Agricultural and Mechanical College of Texas, then known as Texas A.M.C.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brazos_County" title="Brazos County" class="mw-redirect">Brazos County</a> donated 2,416 acres (10&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) near <a href="/wiki/Bryan,_Texas" title="Bryan, Texas">Bryan, Texas</a>, for the school's campus.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Enrollment began on October 2, 1876. Six students enrolled on the first day, and classes officially began on October 4, 1876, with 6 faculty members. During the first semester, enrollment increased to 48 students, and by the end of the spring 1877 semester, 106 students had enrolled. Admission was limited to white males, and all students were required to participate in the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_Corps_of_Cadets" title="Texas A&amp;M University Corps of Cadets">Corps of Cadets</a> and receive military training.<sup id="cite_ref-dethloff1617_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dethloff1617-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Although traditional <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_Corps_of_Cadets" title="Texas A&amp;M University Corps of Cadets">Texas A&amp;M University Corps of Cadets</a> &quot;campusologies&quot; indicate 40 students began classes on October 4, 1876, the exact number of students enrolled on that day is unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Enrollment climbed to 258 students before declining to 108 students in 1883, the year the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas</a> opened in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin, Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Adams16_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adams16-23"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Though originally envisioned and annotated in the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Constitution" title="Texas Constitution" class="mw-redirect">Texas Constitution</a> as a branch of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas" title="University of Texas" class="mw-redirect">University of Texas</a>, Texas A.M.C. had a separate Board of Directors from the University of Texas from the first day of classes and was never enveloped into the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_System" title="University of Texas System">University of Texas System</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:TAMU_Sul_Ross_statue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large domed building overlooks a full-length statue of balding white male with a mustache and long goatee and wearing a knee-length coat. The pedestal is engraved &quot;Lawrence Sullivan Ross&quot;." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/TAMU_Sul_Ross_statue.jpg/170px-TAMU_Sul_Ross_statue.jpg" width="170" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/TAMU_Sul_Ross_statue.jpg/255px-TAMU_Sul_Ross_statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/TAMU_Sul_Ross_statue.jpg/340px-TAMU_Sul_Ross_statue.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:TAMU_Sul_Ross_statue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Statue of 
        <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Sullivan_Ross" title="Lawrence Sullivan Ross">Lawrence Sullivan &quot;Sul&quot; Ross</a> located in front of the Academic Building
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In the late 1880s, many Texas residents saw no need for two colleges in Texas and clamored for an end of Texas A.M.C. In 1891, Texas A&amp;M was saved from potential closure by its new president <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Sullivan_Ross" title="Lawrence Sullivan Ross">Lawrence Sullivan Ross</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Texas" title="Governor of Texas">governor of Texas</a>, and well-respected <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_General" title="Brigadier General" class="mw-redirect">Brigadier General</a>. Ross made many improvements to the school and enrollment doubled to 467 cadets as parents sent their sons to Texas A&amp;M &quot;to learn to be like Ross&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-ferrell_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrell-24"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> During his tenure, many enduring <a href="/wiki/Traditions_of_Texas_A%26M_University" title="Traditions of Texas A&amp;M University">Aggie traditions</a> were born, including the creation of the first <a href="/wiki/Traditions_of_texas_a%26m#Aggie_Ring" title="Traditions of texas a&amp;m" class="mw-redirect">Aggie Ring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ferrell_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrell-24"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> After his death in 1898, a statue was erected in front of what is now Academic Plaza to honor Ross and his achievements in the history of the school.<sup id="cite_ref-ferrell_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrell-24"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Under pressure from the legislature, in 1911 the school began allowing women to attend classes during the summer semester.<sup id="cite_ref-kavanagh_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kavanagh-25"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> At the same time, A&amp;M began expanding its academic pursuits with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_College_of_Veterinary_Medicine_%26_Biomedical_Sciences" title="Texas A&amp;M College of Veterinary Medicine &amp; Biomedical Sciences">School of Veterinary Medicine</a> in 1915.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Wars_era">World Wars era</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: World Wars era">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Many Texas A&amp;M graduates served during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World&nbsp;War&nbsp;I</a>. By 1918, 49% of all graduates of the college were in military service, more than any other school.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> In early September 1918, the entire senior class enlisted, with plans to send the younger students at staggered dates throughout the next year. Many of the seniors were fighting in France when the war ended two months later.<sup id="cite_ref-liffick_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-liffick-26"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Over 1,200&nbsp;former students served as commissioned officers. After the war, Texas A&amp;M grew rapidly and became nationally recognized for its programs in agriculture, engineering, and military science.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> The first graduate school was organized in 1924 and the school awarded its first PhD in 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_19-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1925, Mary Evelyn Crawford Locke became the first female to receive a diploma from Texas A&amp;M, although she was not allowed to participate in the graduation ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-crawford_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crawford-27"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> The following month the Board of Directors officially prohibited all women from enrolling.<sup id="cite_ref-kavanagh_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kavanagh-25"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Many Aggies again served in the military during World War II, with the college producing 20,229 combat troops. Of those, 14,123 Aggies served as officers, more than any other school and more than the combined total of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">United States Naval Academy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-adams160_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adams160-28"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> During the war, 29 A&amp;M graduates reached the rank of general.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_19-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>At the start of World War II, Texas A&amp;M was selected as one of six engineering colleges to participate in the <a href="/wiki/Electronics_Training_Program" title="Electronics Training Program">Electronics Training Program</a>, a 10-month activity of 12-hour study days to train Navy personnel who were urgently needed to maintain the new, highly complex electronic equipment such as <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a>. These colleges provided the Primary School, wherein the key topics of the first two years of a college <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical engineering</a> curriculum were condensed into three months. The instructional effort at College Station was developed and led by Frank Bolton, EE department head and future Texas A&amp;M president. At a given time, some 500 Navy students were on the campus, a significant fraction of the then-years enrollment. Students graduating from the Primary Schools then went to a Secondary School, one of which was at <a href="/wiki/Ward_Island_(Texas)" title="Ward Island (Texas)">Ward Island, Texas</a> (the future location of <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University-Corpus_Christi" title="Texas A&amp;M University-Corpus Christi" class="mw-redirect">Texas A&amp;M University-Corpus Christi</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Enrollment soared after the war as many former soldiers used the <a href="/wiki/G.I._Bill" title="G.I. Bill">G.I. Bill</a> to further their education.<sup id="cite_ref-gillentine_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gillentine-30"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1948, the state Legislature formally recognized Texas A&amp;M as a separate university system from the University of Texas System, codifying the de facto arrangement between the schools.<sup id="cite_ref-system_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-system-31"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="University_era">University era</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: University era">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>On March 26, 1960, <a href="/wiki/Major_General" title="Major General" class="mw-redirect">Major General</a> <a href="/wiki/James_Earl_Rudder" title="James Earl Rudder">James Earl Rudder</a>, class of 1932, became the 16th president of the college.<sup id="cite_ref-dethloff184_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dethloff184-32"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Rudder's tenure (1959?1970) marked a critical turning point in the school's history. Under his leadership, Texas A&amp;M underwent a dramatic expansion in terms of its physical plant construction, but more importantly, it diversified and expanded its student body by admitting women and minorities. The Corps of Cadets became voluntary. In the face of growing student activism during the 1960s, Rudder worked diligently to ensure that school continue to fulfill its mission of providing a quality education for all Aggies. By his death in 1970, Rudder had overseen the growth of the school from 7,500 to 14,000 students from all 50 states and 75 nations.<sup id="cite_ref-rudder_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rudder-33"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1963, the 58th <a href="/wiki/Legislature_of_Texas" title="Legislature of Texas" class="mw-redirect">Legislature of Texas</a> approved of Rudder's changes, and officially renamed the school &quot;Texas A&amp;M University&quot;,<sup id="cite_ref-rudder_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rudder-33"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> specifying that the &quot;A&quot; and the &quot;M&quot; were purely symbolic, reflecting the school's past, and no longer stood for &quot;Agricultural and Mechanical&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_19-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> In the following 35&nbsp;years, Texas A&amp;M more than tripled its enrollment from 14,000&nbsp;students to over 45,000.<sup id="cite_ref-TAMUfacts_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMUfacts-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Much of the legislative work allowing the expansion of Texas A&amp;M and the admission of women was pushed by <a href="/wiki/Texas_State_Senate" title="Texas State Senate" class="mw-redirect">State Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/William_T._Moore_(Texas_politician)" title="William T. Moore (Texas politician)">William T. &quot;Bill&quot; Moore</a>, who served from 1949 to 1981. Known as &quot;the Bull of the Brazos&quot; and &quot;the father of the modern Texas A&amp;M University&quot;, Moore was a Bryan attorney and businessman originally from <a href="/wiki/Wheelock,_Texas" title="Wheelock, Texas">Wheelock</a> in <a href="/wiki/Robertson_County,_Texas" title="Robertson County, Texas">Robertson County</a>. He also taught economics at TAMU prior to his entry into World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Texas A&amp;M became one of the first four universities given the designation <a href="/wiki/Sea-grant_university" title="Sea-grant university" class="mw-redirect">sea-grant</a> for its achievements in <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanography</a> and marine resources development in 1971. In 1989, the university earned the title <a href="/wiki/Space-grant_college" title="Space-grant college" class="mw-redirect">space-grant</a> by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, to recognize its commitment to space research and participation in the Texas Space Grant Consortium.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-36"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:George_Bush_Presidential_Library.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Above the doorway of a large, relatively plain rectangular structure with a short dome are the words &quot;George Bush Library&quot;. In front of the building is a circular courtyard with a water fountain; eight American flags are positioned evenly around the circle." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/George_Bush_Presidential_Library.jpg/220px-George_Bush_Presidential_Library.jpg" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/George_Bush_Presidential_Library.jpg/330px-George_Bush_Presidential_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/George_Bush_Presidential_Library.jpg/440px-George_Bush_Presidential_Library.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:George_Bush_Presidential_Library.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> George Bush Presidential Library
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1997, the school became the home of the <a href="/wiki/George_Bush_Presidential_Library" title="George Bush Presidential Library">George Bush Presidential Library</a>. Operated by the <a href="/wiki/National_Archives_and_Records_Administration" title="National Archives and Records Administration">National Archives and Records Administration</a>, it is one of thirteen American <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">presidential libraries</a>. Former President <a href="/wiki/George_H.W._Bush" title="George H.W. Bush" class="mw-redirect">George Bush</a> remains actively involved with the university, frequently visiting the campus and participating in special events.<sup id="cite_ref-bushlibrary_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bushlibrary-37"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bushlibopens_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bushlibopens-38"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Texas A&amp;M received national media attention on November 18, 1999, when <a href="/wiki/Aggie_Bonfire" title="Aggie Bonfire">Aggie Bonfire</a>, a ninety-year-old student tradition, collapsed during construction. Twelve current and former students died and twenty-seven others were injured. The accident was later attributed to improper design and poor construction practices.<sup id="cite_ref-homeland_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-homeland-39"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The victims' family members filed six lawsuits against Texas A&amp;M officials, the student Bonfire leaders, and the university. Half of the defendants settled their portion of the case in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-kapitan_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kapitan-40"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> and a federal appeals court dismissed the remaining lawsuits against the university in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-vanderwerf_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanderwerf-41"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>With strong support from <a href="/wiki/Rice_University" title="Rice University">Rice University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a> inducted Texas A&amp;M in May 2001, on the basis of the depth of the university's research and academic programs.<sup id="cite_ref-Aggie_Daily_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aggie_Daily-42"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> In December 2006, university President <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gates" title="Robert Gates">Robert Gates</a> resigned from his position to become the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_Defense" title="Secretary of Defense" class="mw-redirect">Secretary of Defense</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-newpresdent_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newpresdent-43"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elsa_Murano" title="Elsa Murano">Elsa Murano</a> became his replacement in January 2008, but later resigned in June 2009<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/R._Bowen_Loftin" title="R. Bowen Loftin">R. Bowen Loftin</a>.</p> 
     <p>Texas A&amp;M left the <a href="/wiki/Big_12_Conference" title="Big 12 Conference">Big 12 Conference</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Conference" title="Southeastern Conference">Southeastern Conference</a> on July 1, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-JoinSEC_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JoinSEC-45"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> This ended Texas A&amp;M's scheduled NCAA athletic competitions with former <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Conference" title="Southwest Conference">Southwest Conference</a> rivals, UT Austin, Baylor, and Texas Tech, for the foreseeable future.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_body">Student body</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Student body">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Bush_Family_Texas_A%26M_Commencement_Dec._12,_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The head and shoulders of three people ? an older man, an older woman, and a middle-aged man ? wearing formal robes are shown in front of a large circular seal. On the outer edges of the seal the letters &quot;XAS A...IVERSITY...87...&quot; are visible; an inner band of leaves separates the letters from a block T superimposed with a star." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Bush_Family_Texas_A%26M_Commencement_Dec._12%2C_2008.jpg/220px-Bush_Family_Texas_A%26M_Commencement_Dec._12%2C_2008.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Bush_Family_Texas_A%26M_Commencement_Dec._12%2C_2008.jpg/330px-Bush_Family_Texas_A%26M_Commencement_Dec._12%2C_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Bush_Family_Texas_A%26M_Commencement_Dec._12%2C_2008.jpg/440px-Bush_Family_Texas_A%26M_Commencement_Dec._12%2C_2008.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Bush_Family_Texas_A%26M_Commencement_Dec._12,_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Then-President 
        <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> smiles with his parents, former President 
        <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>, left, and former First Lady 
        <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Bush" title="Barbara Bush">Barbara Bush</a> following his commencement address at Texas A&amp;M's December 2008 
        <a href="/wiki/Convocation" title="Convocation">convocation</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In the fall 2008 <a href="/wiki/Semester" title="Semester" class="mw-redirect">semester</a>, Texas A&amp;M was the seventh largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_U.S._universities_by_enrollment" title="List of largest U.S. universities by enrollment" class="mw-redirect">American university</a> with an enrollment of 48,039 students pursuing degrees in 10 academic colleges. The student body represents all 50 US states and 130 foreign countries. Texas residents account for 86% of the student population, and 28.9% are either of international origin or members of ethnic <a href="/wiki/Minority_groups" title="Minority groups" class="mw-redirect">minority groups</a>. The student body consists of 46.8% women and 53.2% men.<sup id="cite_ref-TAMU_Office_of_Institutional_Studies_and_Planning_2008_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMU_Office_of_Institutional_Studies_and_Planning_2008-46"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The ratio of white students has been dropped from 77.15% in Fall 1999 <sup id="cite_ref-Enrollment_Profile_1999_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enrollment_Profile_1999-47"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> to 64.2% in Spring 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Enrollment_Profile_Spring_2013_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enrollment_Profile_Spring_2013-48"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> But the inaccurate perception about stereotyping of students still remains.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-blogs.dallasobserver.com_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blogs.dallasobserver.com-50"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-blogs.dallasobserver.com_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blogs.dallasobserver.com-50"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Although Texas A&amp;M is a secular institution, its student body has a reputation for being <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_United_States" title="Religion in the United States">religious</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a>. According to a 2005 student survey published in <a href="/wiki/The_Princeton_Review" title="The Princeton Review">the Princeton Review</a>, Texas A&amp;M ranked 13th highest in the category &quot;students pray on a regular basis&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-Conserv1_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conserv1-56"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Four years later, the Princeton Review ranked Texas A&amp;M the eighth most <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">socially conservative</a> campus in the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-Conserv2_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conserv2-57"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> The Princeton Review also ranked the university in 2012 as the &quot;10th least friendly&quot; college in the United States for <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a> people, and the least friendly among public schools for LGBT people.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university consistently ranks among the top ten public universities each year in enrollment of <a href="/wiki/National_Merit" title="National Merit" class="mw-redirect">National Merit</a> scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-NationalMerit_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalMerit-59"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/College_Board" title="College Board">College Board</a>, the fall 2008 entering freshman class consisted of 54% students in the top 10% of their high school graduating class, 86% in the top quarter, and 99% in the top half.<sup id="cite_ref-CollegeBoard1_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CollegeBoard1-60"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Seventy-four percent of these students took the <a href="/wiki/SAT" title="SAT">SAT</a>. The middle 50% of the freshmen had average scores as follows: in critical reading, 520?630, math, 560?670, and in writing 500?610. Twenty-six percent of the incoming freshmen took the <a href="/wiki/ACT_(examination)" title="ACT (examination)" class="mw-redirect">ACT</a>, with the middle 50% scoring between a 23 and 29.<sup id="cite_ref-CollegeBoard2_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CollegeBoard2-61"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> About 80 percent of the student body receives about $420 million in financial aid annually.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> The admission rate for students who applied as undergraduates in 2012 was 67%.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the fall 2008 semester, the <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Look_College_of_Engineering" title="Dwight Look College of Engineering">Dwight Look College of Engineering</a> had the largest enrollment of 20.5%. The <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_College_of_Liberal_Arts" title="Texas A&amp;M College of Liberal Arts">College of Liberal Arts</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_College_of_Agriculture_and_Life_Sciences" title="Texas A&amp;M College of Agriculture and Life Sciences">College of Agriculture and Life Sciences</a> followed, enrolling 15% and 14% of the student body, respectively. The <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_College_of_Education_and_Human_Development" title="Texas A&amp;M College of Education and Human Development">College of Education and Human Development</a> enrolls 12%, and <a href="/wiki/Mays_Business_School" title="Mays Business School">Mays Business School</a> enrolled about 11%. Colleges with less than 10% enrollment included the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_College_of_Architecture" title="Texas A&amp;M College of Architecture">College of Architecture</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_College_of_Science" title="Texas A&amp;M College of Science">College of Science</a>, the <a href="/wiki/George_Bush_School_of_Government_and_Public_Service" title="George Bush School of Government and Public Service" class="mw-redirect">George Bush School of Government and Public Service</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_College_of_Geosciences" title="Texas A&amp;M College of Geosciences">College of Geosciences</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_College_of_Veterinary_Medicine_%26_Biomedical_Sciences" title="Texas A&amp;M College of Veterinary Medicine &amp; Biomedical Sciences">College of Veterinary Medicine &amp; Biomedical Sciences</a>. Approximately 8% of the student body had not declared a major.<sup id="cite_ref-TAMU_Office_of_Institutional_Studies_and_Planning_2008_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMU_Office_of_Institutional_Studies_and_Planning_2008-46"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the fall 2011 semester, enrollment at Texas A&amp;M surpassed 50,000 for the first time. A record 50,054 students were enrolled on the census day.<sup id="cite_ref-TAMUtimes.28enrollment.29_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMUtimes.28enrollment.29-64"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings">Rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Rankings">edit</a>]</span></h3>

     <p>In the 2013 edition of the <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_and_World_Report" title="U.S. News and World Report" class="mw-redirect">U.S. News and World Report</a></i> ranking of national universities, the school is 65th.<sup id="cite_ref-USNews_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USNews-72"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>Note 3<span>]</span></a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Monthly" title="The Washington Monthly" class="mw-redirect">The Washington Monthly</a></i> criteria, which considers research, community service, and social mobility, Texas A&amp;M ranks second nationally in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-WashingtonMonthly_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WashingtonMonthly-74"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/John_Templeton_Foundation" title="John Templeton Foundation">John Templeton Foundation</a> listed Texas A&amp;M as one of the thirty-five American college programs that &quot;communicate[s] the values of honesty, trust, respect, responsibility, integrity, and fairness in the classroom&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-honestly_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honestly-75"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The 2011 <i><a href="/wiki/Kiplinger%27s_Personal_Finance" title="Kiplinger's Personal Finance">Kiplinger's Personal Finance</a></i> ranked the school as the 23rd best-value public university on the basis of in-state tuition, and the 35th best-value public university on the basis of out-of-state tuition.<sup id="cite_ref-Kiplinger_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kiplinger-76"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> After conducting a survey of leading employment recruiters, the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" title="Wall Street Journal" class="mw-redirect">Wall Street Journal</a> ranked Texas A&amp;M 2nd nationally, as &quot;most likely to help students land a job in key careers and professions&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ1_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ1-77"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2-78"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2009 the National Science Foundation has recognized Texas A&amp;M as one of the top 20 research institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Battalion_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Battalion-79"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Internationally, the university is also well-regarded. In a comparison of educational quality, faculty quality, and research output, <a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Jiao_Tong_University" title="Shanghai Jiao Tong University">Shanghai Jiao Tong University</a> ranked Texas A&amp;M 51st nationally and 93rd internationally in its 2012 rankings.<sup id="cite_ref-shanghai_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shanghai-80"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> In its 2012-2013 rankings, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times_Higher_Education_Supplement" title="The Times Higher Education Supplement" class="mw-redirect">The Times Higher Education Supplement</a></i> listed Texas A&amp;M 67th among North America's universities, and 156th among world universities.<sup id="cite_ref-THES_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-THES-81"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> The 2012/2013 <a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS World University Rankings</a><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> ranked the university 165th overall in the world. In its 2013 edition, the Center for World University Rankings ranked Texas A&amp;M as the 80th university globally and 50th university nationally.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Halbouty.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Branches of a large oak tree shade a sidewalk" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Halbouty.JPG/220px-Halbouty.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Halbouty.JPG/330px-Halbouty.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Halbouty.JPG/440px-Halbouty.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Halbouty.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Michel T. Halbouty Geosciences Building houses the Department of Geology and Geophysics.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Endowment">Endowment</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Endowment">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Texas A&amp;M has an endowment valued at more than $5 billion, which ranks fourth among U.S. public universities and 10th overall.<sup id="cite_ref-tamu.edu_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tamu.edu-84"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Apart from revenue received from tuition and research grants, the university, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_System" title="Texas A&amp;M University System">Texas A&amp;M University System</a> (TAMUS), is partially funded from two endowments. The smaller endowment, totaling $1.17 billion in assets, is run by the private Texas A&amp;M Foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-endowment_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-endowment-5"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> A larger sum is distributed from the Texas <a href="/wiki/Permanent_University_Fund" title="Permanent University Fund">Permanent University Fund</a> (PUF). TAMUS holds a minority stake (one-third) in this fund; the remaining two-thirds belongs to the University of Texas system. As of 2006, the PUF ending net asset value stood at $10.3&nbsp;billion; $400.7&nbsp;million was distributed to the two university systems in fiscal year 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-puf_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-puf-85"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-handbookoftexas_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbookoftexas-86"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> Combined, the total endowment for the TAMUS stands at $7.6&nbsp;billion, as of 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-endowment_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-endowment-5"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Century_Tree.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Century_Tree.jpg/220px-Century_Tree.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Century_Tree.jpg/330px-Century_Tree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Century_Tree.jpg/440px-Century_Tree.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Century_Tree.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Century Tree, a famous campus landmark and subject to several traditions
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Texas A&amp;M University system, in 2006, was the first to explicitly state in its policy that technology commercialization was a criterion that could be used for <a href="/wiki/Tenure" title="Tenure" class="mw-redirect">tenure</a>. Passage of this policy was intended to give faculty more academic freedom and strengthen the university's industry partnerships.<sup id="cite_ref-tenure1_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tenure1-87"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tenure2_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tenure2-88"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Texas A&amp;M works with both state and university agencies on various local and international research projects to forge innovations in science and technology that can have commercial applications. This work is concentrated in two primary locations?Research Valley and Research Park. Research Valley, an alliance of educational and business organizations, consists of 11,400 acres (50&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) with 2,500,000 square feet (232,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) of dedicated research space. An additional 350 acres (1&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>), with 500,000 square feet (46,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) of research space, is located in Research Park.<sup id="cite_ref-ResearchPark_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ResearchPark-89"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Among the school's research entities are the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Institute_for_Genomic_Medicine" title="Texas Institute for Genomic Medicine">Texas Institute for Genomic Medicine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Transportation_Institute" title="Texas Transportation Institute" class="mw-redirect">Texas Transportation Institute</a>, the Cyclotron Institute, the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Biosciences_and_Technology" title="Institute of Biosciences and Technology">Institute of Biosciences and Technology</a>, and the Institute for Plant Genomics and Biotechnology.<sup id="cite_ref-centers_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-centers-90"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> Texas A&amp;M University is a member of the SEC Academic Consortium.</p> 
     <p>In 2011 with $705 million Texas A&amp;M ranked in the top 20 universities for research expenditures; third behind only MIT and UC Berkeley for universities without medical schools.<sup id="cite_ref-tamu.edu_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tamu.edu-84"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2004, Texas A&amp;M System faculty and research submitted 121 new inventions and established 78 new royalty-bearing licensing agreements; the innovations resulted in income of $8 million. The Texas A&amp;M Technology Licensing Office filed for 88 patents for protection of intellectual property in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-Profile_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Profile-91"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Spearheaded by the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_College_of_Veterinary_Medicine_%26_Biomedical_Sciences" title="Texas A&amp;M College of Veterinary Medicine &amp; Biomedical Sciences">College of Veterinary Medicine</a>, Texas A&amp;M scientists created the first cloned domestic animal, a <a href="/wiki/Domestic_cat" title="Domestic cat" class="mw-redirect">cat</a> named 'cc', on December 22, 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-ClonedCat_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClonedCat-92"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Texas A&amp;M was also the first academic institution to <a href="/wiki/Cloning" title="Cloning">clone</a> each of six different species: cattle, a <a href="/wiki/Boer_goat" title="Boer goat">Boer goat</a>, pigs, a cat, a deer and a horse.<sup id="cite_ref-ABC_News_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC_News-93"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2004, Texas A&amp;M joined a consortium of universities to build the <a href="/wiki/Giant_Magellan_Telescope" title="Giant Magellan Telescope">Giant Magellan Telescope</a> in Chile. Estimated to be the largest optical telescope ever constructed, the facility will have seven mirrors, each with a diameter of 8.4 meters (9.2&nbsp;yd). This will give the telescope the equivalent of a 24.5 meters (26.8&nbsp;yd) primary mirror and will be ten times more powerful than the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>. Construction is slated to be complete in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-GMTO_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GMTO-94"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>As part of a collaboration with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Nuclear_Security_Administration" title="National Nuclear Security Administration">National Nuclear Security Administration</a>, Texas A&amp;M completed the first conversion of a <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_Nuclear_Reactors" title="Texas A&amp;M Nuclear Reactors" class="mw-redirect">nuclear research reactor</a> from using highly enriched <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> fuel (70%) to utilizing low-enriched uranium (20%). The eighteen-month project ended on October 13, 2006, after the first ever refueling of the reactor, thus fulfilling a portion of <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">U.S. President</a> <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>?s Global Nuclear Threat Reduction Initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-Nuclear_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear-95"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide">Worldwide</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Worldwide">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:TAMUQ_Profs_and_Students.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Four people standing behind a flag each holding their thumb in the air" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/TAMUQ_Profs_and_Students.jpg/220px-TAMUQ_Profs_and_Students.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4f/TAMUQ_Profs_and_Students.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4f/TAMUQ_Profs_and_Students.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:TAMUQ_Profs_and_Students.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Two professors, a graduate, and an undergraduate student at the 
        <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_at_Qatar" title="Texas A&amp;M University at Qatar">TAMUQ branch campus</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Texas A&amp;M has participated in over 500 research projects in over 80 countries and leads the Southwestern United States in annual research expenditures. The university conducts research on every continent and has formal research and exchange agreements with 100 institutions in 40 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-VicePresidentOfResearch_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VicePresidentOfResearch-96"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> Texas A&amp;M ranks 13th among U.S. research universities in exchange agreements with institutions abroad and student participation in study abroad programs,<sup id="cite_ref-TAMU_Facts_Research_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMU_Facts_Research-97"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> and has strong research collaborations with the National Natural Science Foundation of China and many leading universities in China.<sup id="cite_ref-VicePresidentOfResearch_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VicePresidentOfResearch-96"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Texas A&amp;M owns three international facilities, a multipurpose center in Mexico City, Mexico, the Soltis Research and Education Center near the town of San Isidro, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, and the Santa Chiara Study Abroad Center in <a href="/wiki/Castiglion_Fiorentino" title="Castiglion Fiorentino">Castiglion Fiorentino</a>, Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-santachiara_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-santachiara-98"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CostaRica_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CostaRica-99"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2003, over 1,200 Aggie students, primarily undergraduates, studied abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-InternationalStudies_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InternationalStudies-100"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Marine research occurs an the University's branch campus, <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_at_Galveston" title="Texas A&amp;M University at Galveston">Texas A&amp;M University at Galveston</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Galveston_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galveston-101"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Texas A&amp;M's Center for International Business Studies is one of 28 supported by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Education" title="U.S. Department of Education" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Department of Education</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ED_Gov_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ED_Gov-102"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> The university is also one of only two American universities in partnership with <a href="/wiki/CONACyT" title="CONACyT" class="mw-redirect">CONACyT</a>, Mexico's equivalent of the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a>, to support research in areas including biotechnology, telecommunications, energy, and urban development.<sup id="cite_ref-CONACYT_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CONACYT-103"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, the university is the home of &quot;Las Americas Digital Research Network&quot;, an online architecture network for 26 universities in 12 nations, primarily in Central and South America.<sup id="cite_ref-Las_Americas_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Las_Americas-104"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:TAMUcampus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/TAMUcampus.jpg/220px-TAMUcampus.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/TAMUcampus.jpg/330px-TAMUcampus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/TAMUcampus.jpg/440px-TAMUcampus.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:TAMUcampus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> A view of the main campus, looking north from Kyle Field. At the center is the Academic Building with its copper dome
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Campus_of_Texas_A%26M_University" title="Campus of Texas A&amp;M University">Campus of Texas A&amp;M University</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Texas A&amp;M's <a href="/wiki/College_Station" title="College Station" class="mw-redirect">College Station</a> campus, one of the largest in America, spans 5,200 acres (21&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) plus 350 acres (1&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) for Research Park.<sup id="cite_ref-TAMUCatalog_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMUCatalog-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TAMUfacts_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMUfacts-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> The university is part of the <a href="/wiki/Bryan-College_Station_metropolitan_area" title="Bryan-College Station metropolitan area" class="mw-redirect">Bryan-College Station metropolitan area</a> located within <a href="/wiki/Brazos_County,_Texas" title="Brazos County, Texas">Brazos County</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Brazos_Valley" title="Brazos Valley">Brazos Valley</a> (Southeast Central Texas) region, an area often referred to as &quot;Aggieland&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-aggieland_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aggieland-105"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> According the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census" title="United States Census">U.S. Census Bureau</a>, as of 2008, the population of Brazos County is estimated at 175,122.<sup id="cite_ref-Census_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census-106"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Money_Magazine" title="Money Magazine" class="mw-redirect">Money Magazine</a></i>, in 2006, named College Station the most educated city in Texas, and the 11th most educated American city, due largely to the presence of the university and the size and scope of its research.<sup id="cite_ref-Education_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Education-107"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Aggieland is centrally located within 200 miles (320&nbsp;km) of 3 of the 10 largest cities in the US and 75% of the Texas and <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> populations (approximately 13 million people). The area's major roadway is <a href="/wiki/Texas_State_Highway_6" title="Texas State Highway 6">State Highway 6</a>, and several smaller state highways and <a href="/wiki/Farm_to_Market_Road" title="Farm to Market Road" class="mw-redirect">Farm to Market Roads</a> connect the area to larger highways such as <a href="/wiki/Interstate_45" title="Interstate 45">Interstate 45</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bcschamber_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bcschamber-108"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The campus is bisected by a railroad track operated by <a href="/wiki/Union_Pacific_Railroad" title="Union Pacific Railroad">Union Pacific</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lukejackson_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lukejackson-109"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> The area east of the tracks, known as Main Campus, includes buildings for the colleges of engineering, architecture, geosciences, science, education and liberal arts. Dormitories, as well as the main dining centers and many campus support facilities, are also on Main Campus. Notable buildings on Main Campus include <a href="/wiki/Kyle_Field" title="Kyle Field">Kyle Field</a>, Sterling C. Evans Library, the Academic Building, Harrington Hall, the Memorial Student Center, the Administration Building, Rudder Tower, Albritton Bell Tower, and the <a href="/wiki/Bonfire_Memorial" title="Bonfire Memorial" class="mw-redirect">Bonfire Memorial</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-campusname_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campusname-110"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> To the west of the railroad tracks lies West Campus, which includes most of the sports facilities, the business school, agricultural programs, life sciences, the veterinary college, the political science and economics school, the <a href="/wiki/George_Bush_School_of_Government_and_Public_Service" title="George Bush School of Government and Public Service" class="mw-redirect">George Bush School of Government and Public Service</a>, the <a href="/wiki/George_Bush_Presidential_Library" title="George Bush Presidential Library">George Bush Presidential Library</a> and two schools within the <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_Health_Science_Center" title="Texas A&amp;M Health Science Center">Texas A&amp;M Health Science Center</a>. Research Park, the area of West Campus along <a href="/wiki/Farm_to_Market_Road_60" title="Farm to Market Road 60">Kimbrough Boulevard</a>, includes many research facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-vision2020campus_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vision2020campus-111"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Outside the main campus, the institution formally includes two additional <a href="/wiki/Branch_campus" title="Branch campus" class="mw-redirect">branch campuses</a>: <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_at_Qatar" title="Texas A&amp;M University at Qatar">Texas A&amp;M at Qatar</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Education_City,_Qatar" title="Education City, Qatar" class="mw-redirect">Education City</a> in <a href="/wiki/Doha,_Qatar" title="Doha, Qatar" class="mw-redirect">Doha, Qatar</a> devoted to engineering disciplines<sup id="cite_ref-qatar_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qatar-112"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_at_Galveston" title="Texas A&amp;M University at Galveston">Texas A&amp;M University at Galveston</a> in <a href="/wiki/Galveston,_Texas" title="Galveston, Texas">Galveston, Texas</a>, devoted to marine research and host to the Texas Maritime Academy.<sup id="cite_ref-Galveston_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galveston-101"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> All degrees at the Qatar campus are granted by the university's <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Look_College_of_Engineering" title="Dwight Look College of Engineering">Dwight Look College of Engineering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_School_of_Law" title="Texas A&amp;M School of Law">Texas A&amp;M School of Law</a> is located In Fort Worth, TX.</p> 
     <p>Texas A&amp;M also maintains <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Air_Force_Base" title="Bryan Air Force Base">Texas A&amp;M Riverside Campus</a>, an extension of the main campus, located 10 miles (16&nbsp;km) to the northwest. Unlike Galveston and Qatar, this extension is only a research facility.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_life">Student life</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Student life">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Residential_life">Residential life</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Residential life">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Sbisa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Sbisa.jpg/220px-Sbisa.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Sbisa.jpg/330px-Sbisa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Sbisa.jpg/440px-Sbisa.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Sbisa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Sbisa Dining Hall and central utility plant water tower sporting the greeting, &quot;Welcome to Aggieland&quot;
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>During the 2006 fall semester, 20.5 percent of the student body lived on campus in one of two distinct housing sections located on opposite ends of campus.<sup id="cite_ref-TAMU_Office_of_Institutional_Studies_and_Planning_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAMU_Office_of_Institutional_Studies_and_Planning-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Both the Northside and Southside areas contain student dormitories, also known as residence halls. While some halls are single-sex, others are <a href="/wiki/Co-educational" title="Co-educational" class="mw-redirect">co-educational</a>. Usually students of different genders live on alternate floors, although some halls are segregated by room or suite.<sup id="cite_ref-womack_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-womack-114"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> Residence hall styles vary. Many halls offer only indoor access to individual rooms, but other halls locate room entrances on an outdoor balcony. Room sizes vary by building. Halls with larger rooms include en-suite or private bathrooms, while halls with smaller rooms have a common bathroom on each floor. Several halls include a &quot;substance-free&quot; floor, where residents pledge to avoid bringing <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcohol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use" title="Recreational drug use">drugs</a>, or cigarettes into the hall.<sup id="cite_ref-dorms2002_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dorms2002-115"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Northside consists of 17 student residence halls, including the 3 university honors dorms.<sup id="cite_ref-northside_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northside-116"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Two of the university honors dorms are freshman only. The halls are located near local entertainment district <a href="/wiki/Northgate,_TX" title="Northgate, TX" class="mw-redirect">Northgate</a>, and offer convenient access to campus dining establishments: Sbisa Dining Hall, The Underground and Bernie's Cafe. The demolition of Moore Hall, Crocker Hall, and McInnis Hall is scheduled for August 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> A $58 million 600-bed residence hall will replace the demolished halls that have accommodated roughly the same number. Some halls (particularly Walton) have unofficially claimed tables within the Sbisa Dining Hall and many halls congregate for dinner at a specific time each weekday.<sup id="cite_ref-hixson_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hixson-118"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Several of the residence halls located on Southside are reserved for members of the <a href="#Corps_of_Cadets">Corps of Cadets</a>. Non-corps halls in this area center around the Commons, a hub for activities and dining.<sup id="cite_ref-commons_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-commons-119"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> Southside has two Learning Living Communities, which allow freshmen to live in a cluster with other students who share common interests.<sup id="cite_ref-lllc_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lllc-120"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Facilities for the Corps of Cadets are located in the Quadrangle, or <a href="/wiki/List_of_Texas_Aggie_terms#Q" title="List of Texas Aggie terms" class="mw-redirect">&quot;The Quad&quot;</a>, an area consisting of dormitories, Duncan Dining Hall, and the Corps training fields.<sup id="cite_ref-cadethandbook_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cadethandbook-121"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> The Corps Arches, a series of 12 arches that &quot;[symbolize] the spirit of <a href="#12th_Man">the 12th Man</a> of Texas A&amp;M&quot;, mark the entrance to the Quadrangle.<sup id="cite_ref-arches_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arches-122"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> All cadets, except those who are married or who have had previous military service, must live in the Quad with assigned roommates from the same unit and graduating class. <a href="/wiki/Reveille_(Texas_A%26M)" title="Reveille (Texas A&amp;M)" class="mw-redirect">Reveille</a>, the Aggie <a href="/wiki/Mascot" title="Mascot">mascot</a>, lives with her handlers in the Quadrangle.<sup id="cite_ref-cadetresidenthandbook_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cadetresidenthandbook-123"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corps_of_Cadets">Corps of Cadets</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Texas_A%26M_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Corps of Cadets">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_Corps_of_Cadets" title="Texas A&amp;M University Corps of Cadets">Texas A&amp;M University Corps of Cadets</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:AggieBandFormation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Row of students in uniform with knee-high boots.  Each holds a bugle that dangles a pennant." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/AggieBandFormation.jpg/220px-AggieBandFormation.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/AggieBandFormation.jpg/330px-AggieBandFormation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/AggieBandFormation.jpg/440px-AggieBandFormation.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:AggieBandFormation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Fightin%27_Texas_Aggie_Band" title="Fightin' Texas Aggie Band">Fightin' Texas Aggie Band's</a> Bugle Rank leads the band at halftime at a football game.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Texas A&amp;M is one of six United States <a href="/wiki/Senior_Military_College" title="Senior Military College" class="mw-redirect">Senior Military Colleges</a>. The schools' <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University_Corps_of_Cadets" title="Texas A&amp;M University Corps of Cadets">Corps of Cadets</a> (or <i>the Corps</i>) is one of the largest uniformed student bodies outside the <a href="/wiki/Service_academy" title="Service academy" class="mw-redirect">service academies</a>. Many members participate in <a href="/wiki/ROTC" title="ROTC" class="mw-redirect">ROTC</a> programs and earn commissions in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Armed_Forces" title="United States Armed Forces">United States Armed Forces</a> upon graduation. Members of the Corps have served in every <a href="/wiki/Armed_conflict" title="Armed conflict" class="mw-redirect">armed conflict</a> fought by the United States since 1876. There were 20,229 Aggies who served in World War II, of these, 14,123 served as officers, more than any other school, including the combined totals of the United States Military Academy and the United States Naval Academy. Since 1876, 225 Aggies have served as generals or <a href="/wiki/Flag_Officers" title="Flag Officers" class="mw-redirect">Flag Officers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cadetsconservative_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cadetsconservative-124"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Until 1965, Corps membership was mandatory. The Corps welcomed female members in the fall of 1974,<sup id="cite_ref-nauman_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nauman-125"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> and as of spring 2012, the co-ed Corps boasts an enrollment of more than 2000&nbsp;cadets.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:RobertGates_TAMU_SeniorBoots.JPEG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/RobertGates_TAMU_SeniorBoots.JPEG/220px-RobertGates_TAMU_SeniorBoots.JPEG" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/RobertGates_TAMU_SeniorBoots.JPEG/330px-RobertGates_TAMU_SeniorBoots.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/RobertGates_TAMU_SeniorBoots.JPEG/440px-RobertGates_TAMU_SeniorBoots.JPEG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:RobertGates_TAMU_SeniorBoots.JPEG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gates" title="Robert Gates">Robert Gates</a> and senior cadets from the Corps of Cadets give the &quot;gig 'em&quot; sign at the 
        <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">Pentagon</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Corps is composed of three Air Force Wings, three Army Brigades, two Navy and Marine Regiments, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Fightin%27_Texas_Aggie_Band" title="Fightin' Texas Aggie Band">Fightin' Texas Aggie Band</a>, whose members may be affiliated with any military branch.<sup id="cite_ref-corpssize_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-corpssize-127"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> Parson's Mounted Cavalry is the only mounted ROTC unit in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-ParsonsCav_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ParsonsCav-128"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> The Ross Volunteer Company, the oldest student-run organization in the state, is the official honor guard for the <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Texas" title="Governor of Texas">Governor of Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CorpsAbout_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CorpsAbout-129"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> The Fish Drill Team, a precision, close-order rifle drill team composed entirely of Corps freshmen, represents the school in local and national competitions. They have won the national championship almost every year since their creation in 1946, and have appeared in several Hollywood productions, with prominent roles in the movies <i><a href="/wiki/A_Few_Good_Men_(film)" title="A Few Good Men (film)" class="mw-redirect">A Few Good Men</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Courage_Under_Fire" title="Courage Under Fire">Courage Under Fire</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-fishdrillteam_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fishdrillteam-130"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
